<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png"><img alt="A photograph of the upper body of a man labelled with the names of viruses that infect the different parts" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2263" data-file-width="2368" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png/220px-Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png/330px-Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png/440px-Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viral_infections_and_involved_species.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview of the main types of viral infection and the most notable species involved<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Microbiology_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Microbiology-134">[134]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Examples of common human diseases caused by viruses include the <a href="/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickenpox" title="Chickenpox">chickenpox</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_sores" title="Cold sores">cold sores</a>. Many serious diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebola_virus_disease" title="Ebola virus disease">Ebola virus disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">AIDS (HIV)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avian_influenza" title="Avian influenza">avian influenza</a>, and <a href="/wiki/SARS" title="SARS">SARS</a> are caused by viruses. The relative ability of viruses to cause disease is described in terms of <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a>. Other diseases are under investigation to discover if they have a virus as the causative agent, such as the possible connection between <a href="/wiki/Human_herpesvirus_6" title="Human herpesvirus 6">human herpesvirus 6</a> (HHV6) and neurological diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">multiple sclerosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronic_fatigue_syndrome" title="Chronic fatigue syndrome">chronic fatigue syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID_17276367_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID_17276367-135">[135]</a></sup> There is controversy over whether the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornavirus" title="Bornavirus">bornavirus</a>, previously thought to cause <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurological</a> diseases in horses, could be responsible for <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatric</a> illnesses in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_1999_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_1999-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Viruses have different mechanisms by which they produce disease in an organism, which depends largely on the viral species. Mechanisms at the cellular level primarily include cell lysis, the breaking open and subsequent death of the cell. In <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a>, if enough cells die, the whole organism will start to suffer the effects. Although viruses cause disruption of healthy <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>, resulting in disease, they may exist relatively harmlessly within an organism. An example would include the ability of the <a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex_virus" title="Herpes simplex virus">herpes simplex virus</a>, which causes cold sores, to remain in a dormant state within the human body. This is called latency<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17686862_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17686862-137">[137]</a></sup> and is a characteristic of the herpes viruses, including Epstein–Barr virus, which causes glandular fever, and <a href="/wiki/Varicella_zoster_virus" title="Varicella zoster virus">varicella zoster virus</a>, which causes chickenpox and <a href="/wiki/Shingles" title="Shingles">shingles</a>. Most people have been infected with at least one of these types of herpes virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> These latent viruses might sometimes be beneficial, as the presence of the virus can increase immunity against bacterial pathogens, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Yersinia_pestis" title="Yersinia pestis">Yersinia pestis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some viruses can cause lifelong or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_(medical)" title="Chronic (medical)">chronic</a> infections, where the viruses continue to replicate in the body despite the host's defence mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17931183_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17931183-140">[140]</a></sup> This is common in hepatitis B virus and hepatitis C virus infections. People chronically infected are known as carriers, as they serve as reservoirs of infectious virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17664817_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17664817-141">[141]</a></sup> In populations with a high proportion of carriers, the disease is said to be <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">endemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17645465_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17645465-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h3>
<p>Viral <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiology</a> is the branch of medical science that deals with the transmission and control of virus infections in humans. Transmission of viruses can be vertical, which means from mother to child, or horizontal, which means from person to person. Examples of <a href="/wiki/Vertical_transmission" title="Vertical transmission">vertical transmission</a> include hepatitis B virus and HIV, where the baby is born already infected with the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17825648_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17825648-143">[143]</a></sup> Another, more rare, example is the <a href="/wiki/Varicella_zoster_virus" title="Varicella zoster virus">varicella zoster virus</a>, which, although causing relatively mild infections in children and adults, can be fatal to the foetus and newborn baby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11190597_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11190597-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Horizontal_transmission" title="Horizontal transmission">Horizontal transmission</a> is the most common mechanism of spread of viruses in populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28289251_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28289251-145">[145]</a></sup> Horizontal transmission can occur when body fluids are exchanged during sexual activity, by exchange of saliva or when contaminated food or water is ingested. It can also occur when <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> containing viruses are inhaled or by insect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vectors</a> such as when infected mosquitoes penetrate the skin of a host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28289251_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28289251-145">[145]</a></sup> Most types of viruses are restricted to just one or two of these mechanisms and they are referred to as "respiratory viruses" or "enteric viruses" and so forth. The rate or speed of transmission of viral infections depends on factors that include population density, the number of susceptible individuals, (i.e., those not immune),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15627236_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15627236-146">[146]</a></sup> the quality of healthcare and the weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16544901_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16544901-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Epidemiology is used to break the chain of infection in populations during outbreaks of <a href="/wiki/Viral_disease" title="Viral disease">viral diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Control measures are used that are based on knowledge of how the virus is transmitted. It is important to find the source, or sources, of the outbreak and to identify the virus. Once the virus has been identified, the chain of transmission can sometimes be broken by vaccines. When vaccines are not available, sanitation and disinfection can be effective. Often, infected people are isolated from the rest of the community, and those that have been exposed to the virus are placed in <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> To control the <a href="/wiki/2001_United_Kingdom_foot-and-mouth_outbreak" title="2001 United Kingdom foot-and-mouth outbreak">outbreak</a> of <a href="/wiki/Foot-and-mouth_disease" title="Foot-and-mouth disease">foot-and-mouth disease</a> in cattle in Britain in 2001, thousands of cattle were slaughtered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19091686_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19091686-150">[150]</a></sup> Most viral infections of humans and other animals have <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">incubation periods</a> during which the infection causes no signs or symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Incubation periods for viral diseases range from a few days to weeks, but are known for most infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shorspp_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shorspp-152">[152]</a></sup> Somewhat overlapping, but mainly following the incubation period, there is a period of communicability—a time when an infected individual or animal is contagious and can infect another person or animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shorspp_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shorspp-152">[152]</a></sup> This, too, is known for many viral infections, and knowledge of the length of both periods is important in the control of outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> When outbreaks cause an unusually high proportion of cases in a population, community, or region, they are called epidemics. If outbreaks spread worldwide, they are called <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemics_and_pandemics">Epidemics and pandemics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 flu pandemic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebola_virus_disease" title="Ebola virus disease">Ebola virus disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">List of epidemics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg"><img alt="An electron micrograph of the virus that caused Spanish influenza" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1312" data-file-width="2126" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg/220px-Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg/330px-Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg/440px-Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reconstructed_Spanish_Flu_Virus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">Transmission electron microscope</a> image of a recreated 1918 influenza virus</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> is a worldwide <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 flu pandemic</a>, which lasted until 1919, was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandemic_Severity_Index" title="Pandemic Severity Index">category 5</a> influenza pandemic caused by an unusually severe and deadly influenza A virus. The victims were often healthy young adults, in contrast to most influenza outbreaks, which predominantly affect juvenile, elderly, or otherwise-weakened patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Older estimates say it killed 40–50 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patterson1_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson1-156">[156]</a></sup> while more recent research suggests that it may have killed as many as 100 million people, or 5% of the world's population in 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11875246_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11875246-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although viral pandemics are rare events, HIV—which evolved from viruses found in monkeys and chimpanzees—has been pandemic since at least the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29460740_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29460740-158">[158]</a></sup> During the 20th century there were four pandemics caused by influenza virus and those that occurred in 1918, 1957 and 1968 were severe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30180422_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30180422-159">[159]</a></sup> Most researchers believe that HIV originated in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> during the 20th century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gao_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gao-160">[160]</a></sup> it is now a pandemic, with an estimated 37.9 million people now living with the disease worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAIDS_DATA_2019_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS_DATA_2019-161">[161]</a></sup> There were about 770,000 deaths from AIDS in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAIDS_resources_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS_resources-162">[162]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Joint_United_Nations_Programme_on_HIV/AIDS" title="Joint United Nations Programme on HIV/AIDS">Joint United Nations Programme on HIV/AIDS</a> (UNAIDS) and the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) estimate that AIDS has killed more than 25 million people since it was first recognised on 5 June 1981, making it one of the most destructive epidemics in recorded history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16517997_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16517997-163">[163]</a></sup> In 2007 there were 2.7 million new HIV infections and 2 million HIV-related deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:154px;max-width:154px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ebola_virus_em.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="2887" data-file-width="2043" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ebola_virus_em.png/150px-Ebola_virus_em.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ebola_virus_em.png/225px-Ebola_virus_em.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ebola_virus_em.png/300px-Ebola_virus_em.png 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marburg_virus.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Marburg_virus.jpg/150px-Marburg_virus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Marburg_virus.jpg/225px-Marburg_virus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Marburg_virus.jpg/300px-Marburg_virus.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Ebola (top) and Marburg viruses (bottom)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Several highly lethal viral pathogens are members of the <i><a href="/wiki/Filoviridae" title="Filoviridae">Filoviridae</a></i>. Filoviruses are filament-like viruses that cause <a href="/wiki/Viral_hemorrhagic_fever" title="Viral hemorrhagic fever">viral hemorrhagic fever</a>, and include <a href="/wiki/Ebolavirus" title="Ebolavirus">ebolaviruses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marburgvirus" title="Marburgvirus">marburgviruses</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marburg_virus" title="Marburg virus">Marburg virus</a>, first discovered in 1967, attracted widespread press attention in April 2005 for an outbreak in <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16775337_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16775337-165">[165]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebola_virus_disease" title="Ebola virus disease">Ebola virus disease</a> has also caused <a href="/wiki/List_of_Ebola_outbreaks" title="List of Ebola outbreaks">intermittent outbreaks</a> with high mortality rates since 1976 when it was first identified. The worst and most recent one is the 2013–2016 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebola_virus_epidemic_in_West_Africa" title="Ebola virus epidemic in West Africa">West Africa epidemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Except for smallpox, most pandemics are caused by newly evolved viruses. These <a href="/wiki/Emergent_virus" title="Emergent virus">"emergent"</a> viruses are usually mutants of less harmful viruses that have circulated previously either in humans or other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Severe acute respiratory syndrome (<a href="/wiki/SARS" title="SARS">SARS</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_East_respiratory_syndrome" title="Middle East respiratory syndrome">Middle East respiratory syndrome</a> (MERS)  are caused by new types of <a href="/wiki/Coronavirus" title="Coronavirus">coronaviruses</a>. Other coronaviruses are known to cause mild infections in humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22094080_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22094080-168">[168]</a></sup> so the virulence and rapid spread of SARS infections—that by July 2003 had caused around 8,000 cases and 800 deaths—was unexpected and most countries were not prepared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28475794_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28475794-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>A related coronavirus, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Severe_acute_respiratory_syndrome_coronavirus_2" title="Severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2">severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2 (SARS-Cov-2)</a>, thought to have originated in bats, emerged in <a href="/wiki/Wuhan" title="Wuhan">Wuhan</a>, China in November 2019 and spread rapidly around the world.  Infections with the virus caused the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> that started in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32143502_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32143502-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32093211_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32093211-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32109444_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32109444-171">[171]</a></sup> Unprecedented restrictions in peacetime were placed on international travel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curfews" title="Curfews">curfews</a> were imposed in several major cities worldwide in response to the pandemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oncovirus" title="Oncovirus">Oncovirus</a></div>
<p>Viruses are an established cause of cancer in humans and other species. Viral cancers occur only in a minority of infected persons (or animals). Cancer viruses come from a range of virus families, including both RNA and DNA viruses, and so there is no single type of "<a href="/wiki/Oncovirus" title="Oncovirus">oncovirus</a>" (an obsolete term originally used for acutely transforming retroviruses). The development of cancer is determined by a variety of factors such as host immunity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19467474_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19467474-174">[174]</a></sup> and mutations in the host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18812503_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18812503-175">[175]</a></sup> Viruses accepted to cause human cancers include some genotypes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_virus" title="Hepatitis B virus">hepatitis B virus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C_virus" title="Hepatitis C virus">hepatitis C virus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epstein%E2%80%93Barr_virus" title="Epstein–Barr virus">Epstein–Barr virus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaposi%27s_sarcoma-associated_herpesvirus" title="Kaposi's sarcoma-associated herpesvirus">Kaposi's sarcoma-associated herpesvirus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_T-lymphotropic_virus" title="Human T-lymphotropic virus">human T-lymphotropic virus</a>. The most recently discovered human cancer virus is a polyomavirus (<a href="/wiki/Merkel_cell_polyomavirus" title="Merkel cell polyomavirus">Merkel cell polyomavirus</a>) that causes most cases of a rare form of skin cancer called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merkel_cell_carcinoma" title="Merkel cell carcinoma">Merkel cell carcinoma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19395876_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19395876-176">[176]</a></sup>
Hepatitis viruses can develop into a chronic viral infection that leads to <a href="/wiki/Hepatocellular_carcinoma" title="Hepatocellular carcinoma">liver cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Infection by human T-lymphotropic virus can lead to <a href="/wiki/Tropical_spastic_paraparesis" title="Tropical spastic paraparesis">tropical spastic paraparesis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adult_T-cell_leukemia" title="Adult T-cell leukemia">adult T-cell leukaemia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Human papillomaviruses are an established cause of cancers of <a href="/wiki/Cervix" title="Cervix">cervix</a>, skin, <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Penis" title="Penis">penis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Within the <i><a href="/wiki/Herpesviridae" title="Herpesviridae">Herpesviridae</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Kaposi%27s_sarcoma-associated_herpesvirus" title="Kaposi's sarcoma-associated herpesvirus">Kaposi's sarcoma-associated herpesvirus</a> causes <a href="/wiki/Kaposi%27s_sarcoma" title="Kaposi's sarcoma">Kaposi's sarcoma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primary_effusion_lymphoma" title="Primary effusion lymphoma">body-cavity lymphoma</a>, and Epstein–Barr virus causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burkitt%27s_lymphoma" title="Burkitt's lymphoma">Burkitt's lymphoma</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hodgkin%27s_lymphoma" title="Hodgkin's lymphoma">Hodgkin's lymphoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B</a> <a href="/wiki/Lymphoproliferative_disorders" title="Lymphoproliferative disorders">lymphoproliferative disorder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nasopharyngeal_carcinoma" title="Nasopharyngeal carcinoma">nasopharyngeal carcinoma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Merkel cell polyomavirus closely related to <a href="/wiki/SV40" title="SV40">SV40</a> and mouse polyomaviruses that have been used as animal models for cancer viruses for over 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18449881_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18449881-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Host_defence_mechanisms">Host defence mechanisms</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">Immune system</a></div>
<p>The body's first line of defence against viruses is the <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system" title="Innate immune system">innate immune system</a>. This comprises cells and other mechanisms that defend the host from infection in a non-specific manner. This means that the cells of the innate system recognise, and respond to, pathogens in a generic way, but, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immune system</a>, it does not confer long-lasting or protective immunity to the host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a> is an important innate defence against viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17693253_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17693253-184">[184]</a></sup> Many viruses have a replication strategy that involves double-stranded RNA (dsRNA). When such a virus infects a cell, it releases its RNA molecule or molecules, which immediately bind to a protein complex called a <a href="/wiki/Dicer" title="Dicer">dicer</a> that cuts the RNA into smaller pieces. A biochemical pathway—the <a href="/wiki/RNA-induced_silencing_complex" title="RNA-induced silencing complex">RISC complex</a>—is activated, which ensures cell survival by degrading the viral mRNA. Rotaviruses have evolved to avoid this defence mechanism by not uncoating fully inside the cell, and releasing newly produced mRNA through pores in the particle's inner capsid. Their genomic dsRNA remains protected inside the core of the virion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15579070_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15579070-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15010218_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15010218-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immune system</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> encounters a virus, it produces specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> that bind to the virus and often render it non-infectious. This is called <a href="/wiki/Humoral_immunity" title="Humoral immunity">humoral immunity</a>. Two types of antibodies are important. The first, called <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_M" title="Immunoglobulin M">IgM</a>, is highly effective at neutralising viruses but is produced by the cells of the immune system only for a few weeks. The second, called <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_G" title="Immunoglobulin G">IgG</a>, is produced indefinitely. The presence of IgM in the blood of the host is used to test for acute infection, whereas IgG indicates an infection sometime in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8903801_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8903801-187">[187]</a></sup> IgG antibody is measured when tests for <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> are carried out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9086126_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9086126-188">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antibodies can continue to be an effective defence mechanism even after viruses have managed to gain entry to the host cell. A protein that is in cells, called <a href="/wiki/TRIM21" title="TRIM21">TRIM21</a>, can attach to the antibodies on the surface of the virus particle. This primes the subsequent destruction of the virus by the enzymes of the cell's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteosome" title="Proteosome">proteosome</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21045130_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21045130-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg"><img alt="Two spherical rotavirus particles, one is coated with antibody which looks like many small birds, regularly spaced on the surface of the virus" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="579" data-file-width="1072" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg/220px-Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg/330px-Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg/440px-Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rotavirus_with_antibody.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus" title="Rotavirus">rotaviruses</a>: the one on the right is coated with antibodies that prevent its attachment to cells and infecting them.</div></div></div>
<p>A second defence of vertebrates against viruses is called <a href="/wiki/Cell-mediated_immunity" title="Cell-mediated immunity">cell-mediated immunity</a> and involves immune cells known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_cells" title="T cells">T cells</a>. The body's cells constantly display short fragments of their proteins on the cell's surface, and, if a T cell recognises a suspicious viral fragment there, the host cell is destroyed by 'killer T' cells and the virus-specific T-cells proliferate. Cells such as the <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophage</a> are specialists at this <a href="/wiki/Antigen_presentation" title="Antigen presentation">antigen presentation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17725500_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17725500-190">[190]</a></sup> The production of <a href="/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferon</a> is an important host defence mechanism. This is a hormone produced by the body when viruses are present. Its role in immunity is complex; it eventually stops the viruses from reproducing by killing the infected cell and its close neighbours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11256746_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11256746-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>Not all virus infections produce a protective immune response in this way. HIV evades the immune system by constantly changing the amino acid sequence of the proteins on the surface of the virion. This is known as "escape mutation" as the viral epitopes escape recognition by the host immune response. These persistent viruses evade immune control by sequestration, blockade of <a href="/wiki/Antigen_presentation" title="Antigen presentation">antigen presentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokine</a> resistance, evasion of <a href="/wiki/Natural_killer_cell" title="Natural killer cell">natural killer cell</a> activities, escape from <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antigenic_shift" title="Antigenic shift">antigenic shift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15297608_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15297608-192">[192]</a></sup> Other viruses, called '<a href="/wiki/Neurotropic_virus" title="Neurotropic virus">neurotropic viruses</a>', are disseminated by neural spread where the immune system may be unable to reach them due to <a href="/wiki/Immune_privilege" title="Immune privilege">immune privilege</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25015488_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25015488-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention_and_treatment">Prevention and treatment</span></h3>
<p>Because viruses use vital metabolic pathways within host cells to replicate, they are difficult to eliminate without using drugs that cause toxic effects to host cells in general. The most effective medical approaches to viral diseases are <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccinations</a> to provide immunity to infection, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiviral_drugs" title="Antiviral drugs">antiviral drugs</a> that selectively interfere with viral replication.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccines">Vaccines</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a></div>
<p>Vaccination is a cheap and effective way of preventing infections by viruses. Vaccines were used to prevent viral infections long before the discovery of the actual viruses. Their use has resulted in a dramatic decline in morbidity (illness) and mortality (death) associated with viral infections such as <a href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumps" title="Mumps">mumps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">rubella</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17068034_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17068034-194">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">Smallpox</a> infections have been eradicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16989262_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16989262-195">[195]</a></sup> Vaccines are available to prevent over thirteen viral infections of humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16364754_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16364754-196">[196]</a></sup> and more are used to prevent viral infections of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17892169_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17892169-197">[197]</a></sup> Vaccines can consist of live-attenuated or killed viruses, viral proteins (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigens" title="Antigens">antigens</a>), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_vaccine" title="RNA vaccine">RNA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16494719_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16494719-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid33933145_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid33933145-199">[199]</a></sup> Live vaccines contain weakened forms of the virus, which do not cause the disease but, nonetheless, confer immunity. Such viruses are called attenuated. Live vaccines can be dangerous when given to people with a weak immunity (who are described as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunocompromised" title="Immunocompromised">immunocompromised</a>), because in these people, the weakened virus can cause the original disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1090805_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1090805-200">[200]</a></sup> Biotechnology and genetic engineering techniques are used to produce subunit vaccines. These vaccines use only the capsid proteins of the virus. Hepatitis B vaccine is an example of this type of vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3018891_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3018891-201">[201]</a></sup> Subunit vaccines are safe for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunocompromised" title="Immunocompromised">immunocompromised</a> patients because they cannot cause the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16221073_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16221073-202">[202]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever_vaccine" title="Yellow fever vaccine">yellow fever virus vaccine</a>, a live-attenuated strain called 17D, is probably the safest and most effective vaccine ever generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18564013_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18564013-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiviral_drugs">Antiviral drugs</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">Antiviral drug</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="807" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png/170px-Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png/255px-Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png/340px-Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guanosine-acyclovir-comparison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The structure of the DNA base <a href="/wiki/Guanosine" title="Guanosine">guanosine</a> and the antiviral drug <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acyclovir" title="Acyclovir">acyclovir</a></div></div></div>
<p>Antiviral drugs are often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleoside_analogues" title="Nucleoside analogues">nucleoside analogues</a> (fake DNA building-blocks), which viruses mistakenly incorporate into their genomes during replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27281742_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27281742-204">[204]</a></sup> The life-cycle of the virus is then halted because the newly synthesised DNA is inactive. This is because these analogues lack the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> groups, which, along with <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> atoms, link together to form the strong "backbone" of the DNA molecule. This is called DNA <a href="/wiki/Chain_termination" title="Chain termination">chain termination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15592828_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15592828-205">[205]</a></sup> Examples of nucleoside analogues are <a href="/wiki/Aciclovir" title="Aciclovir">aciclovir</a> for Herpes simplex virus infections and <a href="/wiki/Lamivudine" title="Lamivudine">lamivudine</a> for HIV and hepatitis B virus infections. Aciclovir is one of the oldest and most frequently prescribed antiviral drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid6355051_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid6355051-206">[206]</a></sup>
Other antiviral drugs in use target different stages of the viral life cycle. HIV is dependent on a proteolytic enzyme called the <a href="/wiki/HIV-1_protease" title="HIV-1 protease">HIV-1 protease</a> for it to become fully infectious. There is a large class of drugs called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protease_inhibitors" title="Protease inhibitors">protease inhibitors</a> that inactivate this enzyme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21430338_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21430338-207">[207]</a></sup> There are around thirteen classes of antiviral drugs each targeting different viruses or stages of viral replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27281742_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27281742-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hepatitis C is caused by an RNA virus. In 80% of people infected, the disease is chronic, and without treatment, they are <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infected</a> for the remainder of their lives. There are effective treatments that use <a href="/wiki/Direct-acting_antivirals" title="Direct-acting antivirals">direct-acting antivirals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28319996_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28319996-208">[208]</a></sup> The treatment of chronic <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic_carrier" title="Asymptomatic carrier">carriers</a> of the hepatitis B virus has also been developed by using similar strategies that include lamivudine and other anti-viral drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32102898_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32102898-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infection_in_other_species">Infection in other species</span></h2>
<p>Viruses infect all cellular life and, although viruses occur universally, each cellular species has its own specific range that often infects only that species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Some viruses, called <a href="/wiki/Satellite_(biology)" title="Satellite (biology)">satellites</a>, can replicate only within cells that have already been infected by another virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18690211_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18690211-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_viruses">Animal viruses</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Animal_virus" title="Animal virus">Animal virus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_virology" title="Veterinary virology">Veterinary virology</a></div>
<p>Viruses are important pathogens of livestock. Diseases such as foot-and-mouth disease and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bluetongue" title="Bluetongue">bluetongue</a> are caused by viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18035428_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18035428-211">[211]</a></sup> Companion animals such as cats, dogs, and horses, if not vaccinated, are susceptible to serious viral infections. <a href="/wiki/Canine_parvovirus" title="Canine parvovirus">Canine parvovirus</a> is caused by a small DNA virus and infections are often fatal in pups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> Like all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invertebrates" title="Invertebrates">invertebrates</a>, the honey bee is susceptible to many viral infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Most viruses co-exist harmlessly in their host and cause no signs or symptoms of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimmock_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dimmock-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_viruses">Plant viruses</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plant_virus" title="Plant virus">Plant virus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png/220px-Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png/330px-Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png/440px-Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pepper_mild_mottle_virus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Capsicum" title="Capsicum">Peppers</a> infected by mild mottle virus</div></div></div>
<p>There are many types of plant viruses, but often they cause only a loss of <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">yield</a>, and it is not economically viable to try to control them. Plant viruses are often spread from plant to plant by organisms, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vectors</a>. These are usually insects, but some fungi, <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode worms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">single-celled organisms</a>, and parasitic plants are vectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> When control of plant virus infections is considered economical, for perennial fruits, for example, efforts are concentrated on killing the vectors and removing alternate hosts such as weeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Plant viruses cannot infect humans and other animals because they can reproduce only in living plant cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>Originally from Peru, the potato has become a staple crop worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24925679_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24925679-217">[217]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Potato_virus_Y" title="Potato virus Y">potato virus Y</a> causes disease in potatoes and related species including tomatoes and peppers. In the 1980s, this virus acquired economical importance when it proved difficult to control in seed potato crops. Transmitted by <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>, this virus can reduce crop yields by up to 80 per cent, causing significant losses to potato yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31559020_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31559020-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plants have elaborate and effective defence mechanisms against viruses. One of the most effective is the presence of so-called resistance (R) genes. Each R gene confers resistance to a particular virus by triggering localised areas of cell death around the infected cell, which can often be seen with the unaided eye as large spots. This stops the infection from spreading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> RNA interference is also an effective defence in plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> When they are infected, plants often produce natural disinfectants that kill viruses, such as <a href="/wiki/Salicylic_acid" title="Salicylic acid">salicylic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen molecules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plant virus particles or virus-like particles (VLPs) have applications in both <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>. The capsids of most plant viruses are simple and robust structures and can be produced in large quantities either by the infection of plants or by expression in a variety of heterologous systems. Plant virus particles can be modified genetically and chemically to encapsulate foreign material and can be incorporated into supramolecular structures for use in biotechnology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LomonossoffGP_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-LomonossoffGP-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial_viruses">Bacterial viruses</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">Bacteriophage</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phage.jpg"><img alt="An electron micrograph showing a portion of a bacterium covered with viruses" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1733" data-file-width="1480" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Phage.jpg/170px-Phage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Phage.jpg/255px-Phage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Phage.jpg/340px-Phage.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transmission electron micrograph of multiple bacteriophages attached to a bacterial cell wall</div></div></div>
<p>Bacteriophages are a common and diverse group of viruses and are the most abundant biological entity in aquatic environments—there are up to ten times more of these viruses in the oceans than there are bacteria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> reaching levels of 250,000,000 bacteriophages per millilitre of seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> These viruses infect specific bacteria by binding to <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">surface receptor molecules</a> and then entering the cell. Within a short amount of time, in some cases, just minutes, bacterial <a href="/wiki/Polymerase" title="Polymerase">polymerase</a> starts translating viral mRNA into protein. These proteins go on to become either new virions within the cell, helper proteins, which help assembly of new virions, or proteins involved in cell lysis. Viral enzymes aid in the breakdown of the cell membrane, and, in the case of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T4_phage" title="T4 phage">T4 phage</a>, in just over twenty minutes after injection over three hundred phages could be released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>The major way bacteria defend themselves from bacteriophages is by producing enzymes that destroy foreign DNA. These enzymes, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Restriction_endonucleases" title="Restriction endonucleases">restriction endonucleases</a>, cut up the viral DNA that bacteriophages inject into bacterial cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> Bacteria also contain a system that uses <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a> sequences to retain fragments of the genomes of viruses that the bacteria have come into contact with in the past, which allows them to block the virus's replication through a form of <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> This genetic system provides bacteria with <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">acquired immunity</a> to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27234458_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27234458-229">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeal_viruses">Archaeal viruses</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archaeal_virus" title="Archaeal virus">Archaeal virus</a></div>
<p>Some viruses replicate within <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>: these are DNA viruses with unusual and sometimes unique shapes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrence_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prangishvili_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prangishvili-69">[69]</a></sup> These viruses have been studied in most detail in the <a href="/wiki/Thermophile" title="Thermophile">thermophilic</a> archaea, particularly the orders <a href="/wiki/Sulfolobales" title="Sulfolobales">Sulfolobales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermoproteales" title="Thermoproteales">Thermoproteales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> Defences against these viruses involve RNA interference from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Repetitive_DNA" title="Repetitive DNA">repetitive DNA</a> sequences within archaean genomes that are related to the genes of the viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> Most archaea have CRISPR–Cas systems as an adaptive defence against viruses. These enable archaea to retain sections of viral DNA, which are then used to target and eliminate subsequent infections by the virus using a process similar to RNA interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24909109_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24909109-233">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_aquatic_ecosystems">Role in aquatic ecosystems</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_virus" title="Marine virus">Marine virus</a></div>
<p>Viruses are the most abundant biological entity in aquatic environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16984643_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16984643-2">[2]</a></sup> There are about ten million of them in a teaspoon of seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31749771_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31749771-234">[234]</a></sup> Most of these viruses are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacteriophages" title="Bacteriophages">bacteriophages</a> infecting heterotrophic bacteria and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyanophages" title="Cyanophages">cyanophages</a> infecting cyanobacteria and they are essential to the regulation of saltwater and freshwater ecosystems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30443861_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30443861-235">[235]</a></sup>
Bacteriophages are harmless to plants and animals, and are essential to the regulation of marine and freshwater ecosystems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>  are important mortality agents of <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>, the base of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foodchain" title="Foodchain">foodchain</a> in aquatic environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16163346_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16163346-237">[237]</a></sup> They infect and destroy bacteria in aquatic microbial communities, and are one of the most important mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">recycling carbon</a> and nutrient cycling in marine environments. The organic molecules released from the dead bacterial cells stimulate fresh bacterial and algal growth, in a process known as the <a href="/wiki/Viral_shunt" title="Viral shunt">viral shunt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> In particular, lysis of bacteria by viruses has been shown to enhance nitrogen cycling and stimulate phytoplankton growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup>  Viral activity may also affect the <a href="/wiki/Biological_pump" title="Biological pump">biological pump</a>, the process whereby <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">sequestered</a> in the deep ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17853907_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17853907-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p>Microorganisms constitute more than 90% of the biomass in the sea. It is estimated that viruses kill approximately 20% of this biomass each day and that there are 10 to 15 times as many viruses in the oceans as there are bacteria and archaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27572161_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27572161-241">[241]</a></sup> Viruses are also major agents responsible for the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> including <a href="/wiki/Harmful_algal_bloom" title="Harmful algal bloom">harmful algal blooms</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup>
The number of viruses in the oceans decreases further offshore and deeper into the water, where there are fewer host organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17853907_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17853907-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2018, scientists reported that 800 million viruses, mainly of marine origin, are deposited daily from the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> onto every square meter of the planet's surface, as the result of a global atmospheric stream of viruses, circulating above the weather system but below the altitude of usual airline travel, distributing viruses around the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180413_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180413-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISME-2018_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISME-2018-244">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like any organism, <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a> are susceptible to viral infections. In 1988 and 2002, thousands of <a href="/wiki/Harbor_seal" title="Harbor seal">harbour seals</a> were killed in Europe by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phocine_distemper_virus" title="Phocine distemper virus">phocine distemper virus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> Many other viruses, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calicivirus" title="Calicivirus">caliciviruses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpesvirus" title="Herpesvirus">herpesviruses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenovirus" title="Adenovirus">adenoviruses</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parvovirus" title="Parvovirus">parvoviruses</a>, circulate in marine mammal populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17853907_240-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17853907-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_evolution">Role in evolution</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">Horizontal gene transfer</a></div>
<p>Viruses are an important natural means of transferring genes between different species, which increases <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> and drives evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canchaya_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canchaya-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broecker2019_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broecker2019-246">[246]</a></sup> It is thought that viruses played a central role in early evolution, before the diversification of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_universal_ancestor" title="Last universal ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> into bacteria, archaea and eukaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11536914_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11536914-247">[247]</a></sup> Viruses are still one of the largest reservoirs of unexplored genetic diversity on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17853907_240-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17853907-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_sciences_and_medicine">Life sciences and medicine</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_virus_research.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2786" data-file-width="1966" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/170px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/255px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/340px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_virus_research.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scientist studying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H5N1" title="H5N1">H5N1</a> influenza virus</div></div></div>
<p>Viruses are important to the study of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cell biology</a> as they provide simple systems that can be used to manipulate and investigate the functions of cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> The study and use of viruses have provided valuable information about aspects of cell biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> For example, viruses have been useful in the study of <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> and helped our understanding of the basic mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_processing" title="RNA processing">RNA processing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translation_(genetics)" title="Translation (genetics)">translation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> transport, and <a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">immunology</a>.
</p><p>Geneticists often use viruses as <a href="/wiki/Vector_(molecular_biology)" title="Vector (molecular biology)">vectors</a> to introduce genes into cells that they are studying. This is useful for making the cell produce a foreign substance, or to study the effect of introducing a new gene into the genome. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Virotherapy" title="Virotherapy">virotherapy</a> uses viruses as vectors to treat various diseases, as they can specifically target cells and DNA. It shows promising use in the treatment of cancer and in <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a>. Eastern European scientists have used <a href="/wiki/Phage_therapy" title="Phage therapy">phage therapy</a> as an alternative to antibiotics for some time, and interest in this approach is increasing, because of the high level of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a> now found in some pathogenic bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16258815_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16258815-250">[250]</a></sup>
The expression of heterologous proteins by viruses is the basis of several manufacturing processes that are currently being used for the production of various proteins such as vaccine <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> and antibodies. Industrial processes have been recently developed using viral vectors and several pharmaceutical proteins are currently in pre-clinical and clinical trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlebaYY_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlebaYY-251">[251]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Virotherapy">Virotherapy</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Virotherapy" title="Virotherapy">Virotherapy</a></div>
<p>Virotherapy involves the use of genetically modified viruses to treat diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> Viruses have been modified by scientists to reproduce in cancer cells and destroy them but not infect healthy cells. <a href="/wiki/Talimogene_laherparepvec" title="Talimogene laherparepvec">Talimogene laherparepvec</a> (T-VEC), for example, is a modified <a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex_virus" title="Herpes simplex virus">herpes simplex virus</a> that has had a gene, which is required for viruses to replicate in healthy cells, deleted and replaced with a human gene (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GM-CSF" title="GM-CSF">GM-CSF</a>) that stimulates immunity. When this virus infects cancer cells, it destroys them and in doing so the presence the GM-CSF gene attracts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dendritic_cells" title="Dendritic cells">dendritic cells</a> from the surrounding tissues of the body. The dendritic cells process the dead cancer cells and present components of them to other cells of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> Having completed successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_trials" title="Clinical trials">clinical trials</a>, the virus gained approval for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Melanoma" title="Melanoma">melanoma</a> in late 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> Viruses that have been reprogrammed to kill cancer cells are called <a href="/wiki/Oncolytic_virus" title="Oncolytic virus">oncolytic viruses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials_science_and_nanotechnology">Materials science and nanotechnology</span></h3>
<p>Current trends in nanotechnology promise to make much more versatile use of viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> From the viewpoint of a materials scientist, viruses can be regarded as organic nanoparticles.
Their surface carries specific tools that enable them to cross the barriers of their host cells. The size and shape of viruses and the number and nature of the functional groups on their surface are precisely defined. As such, viruses are commonly used in materials science as scaffolds for covalently linked surface modifications. A particular quality of viruses is that they can be tailored by directed evolution. The powerful techniques developed by life sciences are becoming the basis of engineering approaches towards nanomaterials, opening a wide range of applications far beyond biology and medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fischlechner_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-fischlechner-257">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of their size, shape, and well-defined chemical structures, viruses have been used as templates for organising materials on the nanoscale. Recent examples include work at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_Research_Laboratory" title="Naval Research Laboratory">Naval Research Laboratory</a> in Washington, D.C., using <a href="/wiki/Cowpea_mosaic_virus" title="Cowpea mosaic virus">Cowpea mosaic virus</a> (CPMV) particles to amplify signals in <a href="/wiki/DNA_microarray" title="DNA microarray">DNA microarray</a> based sensors. In this application, the virus particles separate the <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescent</a> <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyes</a> used for signalling to prevent the formation of non-fluorescent <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimers</a> that act as <a href="/wiki/Quenching_(fluorescence)" title="Quenching (fluorescence)">quenchers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> Another example is the use of CPMV as a nanoscale breadboard for molecular electronics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthetic_viruses">Synthetic viruses</span></h3>
<p>Many viruses can be synthesised de novo ("from scratch").  The first synthetic virus was created in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> Although somewhat of a misconception, it is not the actual virus that is synthesised, but rather its DNA genome (in case of a DNA virus), or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CDNA" title="CDNA">cDNA</a> copy of its genome (in case of RNA viruses). For many virus families the naked synthetic DNA or RNA (once enzymatically converted back from the synthetic cDNA) is infectious when introduced into a cell. That is, they contain all the necessary information to produce new viruses. This technology is now being used to investigate novel vaccine strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coleman_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coleman-261">[261]</a></sup> The ability to synthesise viruses has far-reaching consequences, since viruses can no longer be regarded as extinct, as long as the information of their genome sequence is known and <a href="/wiki/Permissive" title="Permissive">permissive</a> cells are available. As of June 2021, the full-length genome sequences of 11,464 different viruses, including smallpox, are publicly available in an online database maintained by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weapons">Weapons</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">Biological warfare</a></div>
<p>The ability of viruses to cause devastating epidemics in human societies has led to the concern that viruses could be weaponised for <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">biological warfare</a>. Further concern was raised by the successful recreation of the infamous <a href="/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">1918 influenza</a> virus in a laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30152475_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30152475-263">[263]</a></sup>
The smallpox virus devastated numerous societies throughout history before its eradication. There are only two centres in the world authorised by the WHO to keep stocks of smallpox virus: the <a href="/wiki/State_Research_Center_of_Virology_and_Biotechnology_VECTOR" title="State Research Center of Virology and Biotechnology VECTOR">State Research Center of Virology and Biotechnology VECTOR</a> in Russia and the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18844596_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18844596-264">[264]</a></sup> It may be used as a weapon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18844596_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18844596-264">[264]</a></sup> as the vaccine for smallpox sometimes had severe side-effects, it is no longer used routinely in any country. Thus, much of the modern human population has almost no established resistance to smallpox and would be vulnerable to the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18844596_264-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18844596-264">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
